The attack lasted about an hour. During this time, the hacker sold approximately 2,609 Bitcoin and received about $3,000 in cash, then attempted to withdraw these funds. But Mt. Gox's withdrawal system had delays, and the hacker only successfully withdrew part of the funds.

Karpeles took emergency measures. He froze all withdrawals, shut down the trading system, and began investigating the losses. He found that though the price had crashed, most transactions could be "rolled back" — the hacker's transactions during the attack could be reversed, restoring the system to its pre-attack state.

"The rollback was controversial," Karpeles said. "In traditional financial markets, transactions are irreversible. But Bitcoin is different. If we didn't roll back, many users would lose large amounts of money. We had to protect them."

Mt. Gox rolled back the transactions during the attack, stripping the profits of those lucky buyers who had purchased Bitcoin at $0.01. This caused some controversy — those buyers complained they hadn't done anything wrong, so why should they be denied their profits? But most people understood it was a necessary measure to prevent system collapse.

### II. The Vulnerability of the System

The attack exposed serious vulnerabilities in Mt. Gox's system.

First, the security architecture had fundamental flaws. Administrator passwords should never be stored on any internet-connected computer. There should have been strict access controls, multi-factor authentication, and comprehensive audit logs. Mt. Gox had none of these.

Second, the trade matching mechanism had vulnerabilities. The system should not have allowed prices to plummet 99% in an instant — there should have been circuit breakers to prevent extreme volatility. Mt. Gox had no such protections.

Third, risk management was absent. Mt. Gox had no emergency response plan, no crisis management process, no backup systems. When the attack occurred, Karpeles could only respond by instinct, with no time to consider the best course of action.

"We were a system held together with duct tape and string," a former employee later admitted. "It worked under normal conditions, but against an attack it was like a house of cards. I'm surprised we weren't breached sooner."

The attack also exposed Karpeles's personal management problems. He tried to do everything himself, didn't trust others, and hadn't built an effective team. This created a single point of failure — if Karpeles's computer was breached, the entire system was exposed.

"Mark was a control freak," a friend said. "He wanted to control everything and didn't want to delegate to anyone. This might have worked in the early days, but when the company grew, it became a liability. One person can't manage a global exchange — it's not possible."

### III. Cracks in Trust

Although Mt. Gox successfully rolled back the attack, users' trust had already begun to erode.

"If hackers could so easily breach the system," one user wrote on the forum, "are our funds even safe? What if the next attack is even worse? What if Mark can't stop it?"

There were no answers to these questions. Karpeles promised to strengthen security, hire more technical staff, and improve the system architecture. But users no longer fully trusted him. Some began transferring their Bitcoin from Mt. Gox to personal wallets, reducing their exposure to exchange risk.

"I started to worry," a long-time user said. "Mt. Gox looked too big, too important to fail. But what if it did fail? What would happen to the Bitcoin I had deposited there?"

These concerns were justified. Mt. Gox held users' Bitcoin, but these Bitcoin were actually controlled by Mt. Gox. Users only had digital records of account balances, not the private keys to their Bitcoin. If Mt. Gox went bankrupt or was hacked, users might never recover their funds.

"This is the fundamental problem with centralized exchanges," a cryptographer warned. "You entrust your assets to a third party, trusting they will take good care of them. But history tells us this trust is often misplaced. Banks fail, exchanges get hacked, funds disappear. Bitcoin was invented to avoid this, but we've returned to square one."

### IV. The Significance of Omens

The June 2011 hack was an omen of Mt. Gox's fate. It presaged an even larger collapse to come, though at the time no one realized it.

"If we had seriously investigated back then," a security expert later commented, "we would have immediately shut down Mt. Gox and demanded Karpeles undergo a comprehensive audit and establish effective oversight. But we didn't. The Bitcoin community was too excited, too focused on rising prices, too trusting of Mark Karpeles. We ignored the warning signs."

The warning signs continued to appear. In 2012, Mt. Gox's bank accounts were frozen by the U.S. government. In 2013, withdrawal delays became the norm, with users complaining they had to wait weeks to withdraw funds. With each crisis, Karpeles managed to survive, but Mt. Gox's reputation was gradually damaged and user trust was gradually eroding.

"It was a slow death," an observer said. "Mt. Gox was like a leaking ship, with the captain constantly bailing water with buckets, but the water kept coming in. Eventually, the ship would sink — it was just a matter of time."

The ship finally sank in 2014. Mt. Gox filed for bankruptcy, admitting to the loss of 850,000 Bitcoin, worth approximately $450 million at the time. Karpeles was arrested and charged with embezzlement and fraud. Mt. Gox became the greatest disaster in Bitcoin history, an eternal warning about greed, incompetence, and the dangers of misplaced trust.

But in 2011, all of this was still in the future. Mt. Gox continued operating, Bitcoin continued thriving, and people continued believing. The omens were ignored, the warnings were forgotten, and the feast continued. Until the final collapse, people would recall that phone call in the early morning of June 2011, that instantaneous price crash, that first dark arrow of fate.

### V. Reflection: The Absurdity of Value and the Inferno of Speculation

Laszlo Hanyecz's 10,000 Bitcoin pizza, Mark Karpeles's Mt. Gox empire, the 2011 price surge and hacking crisis — these stories together constitute the chronicle of the crypto world's early days. What do they tell us?

First, value is absurd. In 2010, 10,000 Bitcoin were worth two pizzas. In 2017, they were worth $200 million. The pizza was the same pizza, Bitcoin was the same Bitcoin, but the value changed by tens of millions of times. This reminds us that market prices do not always reflect true value — value is often merely the product of collective hallucination.

"Value is like flowers in a mirror or water in the moon's reflection," a poet wrote. "It exists, and yet does not. It is real, and yet illusory. We see in it what we want to see — wealth, hope, fear, greed. It is the projection of human psychology, the crystallization of social consensus."

Second, speculation is dangerous. The 2011 price surge attracted countless people into Bitcoin, and most of them were wiped out. Those who bought at high prices suffered heavily in the crash. Those who borrowed money to invest fell into debt crises. Those driven by FOMO became the last bag holders.

"Speculation is the fire of hell," a philosopher warned. "It burns everything — reason, caution, morality. It promises wealth but brings destruction. It drives people mad, blinds them, and makes them slaves to their own greed."

But most importantly, these stories remind us that innovation always comes with risk. Bitcoin was a revolutionary technology, but it also brought new dangers — hacking attacks, exchange collapses, price volatility. We cannot abandon innovation because of risk, but neither can we ignore risk in blind pursuit of returns.

"The crypto world is a marketplace," an old user summarized. "There are heroes, there are villains, there are fortunes made and fortunes lost. It is an amplifier of human nature, a battlefield of greed and idealism. We see ourselves in it — our hopes, our fears, our strengths, our weaknesses."

Laszlo Hanyecz spent his Bitcoin on pizza and became part of history. Mark Karpeles built an empire and watched it collapse. Countless speculators flooded into the market; some got rich, some went broke. This is the turbulence of the crypto world — an endless story, an eternal cycle.

"The storm never ceases," the old man in the preface had said. "Only time will tell who laughs last."

In the summer of 2011, the storm was just beginning to show, the feast was still going on, and the first arrow had already been loosed. The next chapter will be the rise of the mining revolution, the legend of烤猫's rise, and another story about genius and disappearance.

But that is another story for another time. For now, let us remember Laszlo Hanyecz, remember his pizza, remember his idealism. Behind hundreds of millions of dollars' worth of Bitcoin is the aroma of two pizzas, the dream of a programmer, the most expensive dinner in crypto history.

"I don't regret it," Laszlo said many years later. "I did what I believed was right. History will judge me, but I can face that judgment with a clear conscience. I participated in history, I changed the world — even if only in a small way. That's worth more than any wealth."

This is the price of pizza.

---

**End of Chapter**

---

*Note: The historical events, characters, and details in this chapter are based on real history. Laszlo Hanyecz's pizza transaction, Mark Karpeles's Mt. Gox exchange, and the 2011 price surge and hacking attacks are all historical facts. Some dialogues and inner monologues are fictionalized for literary purposes.*
# Chapter 4: The Mining Machine Revolution — The Rise and Disappearance of Friedcat

> A technical genius blazed across the sky like a shooting star, igniting a mining revolution—only to plunge into the abyss at its peak. The fatal paradox of anonymity and trust; the "disappearances" in the crypto world haunt like ghosts, warning of greed's power to devour.

---

## Prologue: The Awakening of a Genius (2011–2012)

### I. The Flash of the Youth Class at USTC

Hefei, 2011. The summer sun blazed with ferocious heat. In a dormitory room of the Special Class for the Gifted Young at the University of Science and Technology of China, an old desktop computer hummed softly, its blue screen glow illuminating a young, slender face.

Jiang Xinyu stared at the numbers dancing across the screen, his fingers drumming unconsciously on the desk.

"Bitcoin……" he murmured the name, his voice hoarse.

As a prodigy of the Special Class for the Gifted Young at USTC, Jiang Xinyu had long been accustomed to thinking differently from ordinary people. While his peers were sweating over final exams, he had already completed his undergraduate coursework in computer science. When his classmates began pursuing master's degrees, he had already received an admission offer from Yale University's PhD program. In everyone's eyes, he was an undisputed genius—the boy who could solve complex differential equations in three minutes, the prodigy admitted to a top university at seventeen.

But at this moment, the concept of this decentralized digital currency on the screen filled this widely acknowledged genius with an unprecedented tremor.

"No central bank, no government backing, value maintained entirely through cryptographic principles and a distributed network……" Jiang Xinyu pushed his black-framed glasses up the bridge of his nose. "This is truly a perfect crystallization of mathematics and cryptography."

He stood up and paced the cramped dormitory room. In the six-square-meter space, a bed, a desk, and a computer occupied nearly all the room. But his mind had long since soared beyond this confined physical space, entering an entirely new dimension.

He returned to the computer, opened the Bitcoin forum, and began reading every technical document with ravenous hunger.

He had studied Satoshi Nakamoto's whitepaper no fewer than ten times, and each reading revealed new details. Those seemingly dry mathematical formulas and technical descriptions were as beautiful as poetry in his eyes.

"Proof of work…… hash computation…… blockchain……" These terms wove together in his mind into a grand vision.

Suddenly, an idea struck him like lightning.

Jiang Xinyu leapt to his feet, his knee slamming into the corner of the desk, but he felt nothing. His heart raced; blood surged through his veins.

"If we could design a chip specifically for Secure Hash Algorithm 256-bit (SHA-256) computation, wouldn't mining efficiency increase by hundreds or even thousands of times?"

At the time, Bitcoin mining was still停留在 the era of Central Processing Units (CPU), with Graphics Processing Unit (GPU) mining only just emerging. Some Field-Programmable Gate Array (FPGA) miners already existed on the market, but the efficiency gains were limited. Most people believed GPU mining was already sufficiently efficient, with little room for further optimization.

But Jiang Xinyu saw a much farther vision—Application-Specific Integrated Circuits (ASIC) would completely revolutionize this industry. ASICs are chips designed for specific applications. Unlike general-purpose processors, they could be极致 optimized for particular algorithms, achieving performance and energy efficiency levels that general-purpose processors could never reach.

"A CPU is a general-purpose computing device, designed to handle all manner of tasks, so its efficiency on any specific algorithm is necessarily limited." He spoke to himself, his voice trembling with excitement. "While GPUs have strong parallel processing capabilities, they are fundamentally designed for graphics rendering—a lot of transistors are wasted on functions unrelated to mining."

He walked to the window and pushed it open, letting the heat flood into the room. In the distance, Hefei's nightscape was unfolding, ten thousand lights glittering like stars on the ground.

"Only ASICs," he continued. "Only chips optimized specifically for the SHA-256 algorithm can unleash the greatest computational potential. If we could design a chip that devotes every single transistor to hash computation, the efficiency would be hundreds or thousands of times that of GPUs!"

The breathtaking nature of this idea lay not just in its potential to transform the technical landscape of Bitcoin mining, but in its power to reshape the entire industry's power structure. Whoever controlled the most advanced mining technology would control the Bitcoin network's hashrate, and thus control the话语权.

Jiang Xinyu returned to the computer and opened a blank document, beginning to sketch out the technical blueprint for an ASIC miner.

"The project codename……" He paused, his gaze falling on a stuffed toy cat in the corner of the laboratory—a memento left by his ex-girlfriend, a chubby orange kitten that had earned the name "Friedcat" because he often held it while thinking.

Friedcat. 烤猫.

The setting sun painted the sky blood-red beyond the window. In that ordinary dormitory room, a plan that would soon shock the entire cryptocurrency world was quietly taking shape.

---

### II. The Lonely Years at Yale

Fall 2011, New Haven, Connecticut, USA.

The Yale University campus was ablaze with golden maple leaves, its ancient Gothic architecture gleaming in the sunlight. But Jiang Xinyu had no mind for the scenery. His entire energy was immersed in the world of Bitcoin, sleeping only three or four hours a day, spending every other moment studying Bitcoin source code and chip design documentation.

The doctoral coursework came easily to him. Those complex algorithmic theories and mathematical models were child's play in his eyes. He spent most of his time holed up in the laboratory, sailing through an ocean of code.

The laboratory was located in the basement of the Computer Science building, with no windows, only fluorescent lights casting their pallid glow. The walls were plastered with circuit diagrams and algorithm flowcharts; the desks were piled high with empty coffee cups and takeout containers.

"Xinyu, you've been up all night again?"

The laboratory door swung open, and a blue-eyed, blonde-haired American classmate poked his head in. His name was Mike, a fellow lab member of Jiang Xinyu's.

Mike looked at Jiang Xinyu's bloodshot eyes and disheveled hair, shaking his head helplessly. Jiang Xinyu's hair hadn't been washed in days, greasy and plastered to his forehead; his T-shirt reeked of sweat.

"Got a lead—can't stop." Jiang Xinyu didn't look up, his fingers still tapping on the keyboard. The screen scrolled with dense lines of code—the Bitcoin Core client source.

"What on earth are you working on?" Mike walked into the lab, pulled up a chair, and sat down. "The professor says you've been slacking off on your coursework lately, and you haven't shown up to group meetings. He's not happy."

Jiang Xinyu stopped working and turned around. There was something in his eyes that Mike found unfamiliar—a light of fervor, of faith, of ecstasy upon discovering an entirely new world.

"I'm researching the future." He spoke simply, his voice hoarse but full of power. "A brand-new form of currency, a technology that could change the world."

Mike furrowed his brow. He knew Jiang Xinyu was a genius, but he also knew that geniuses are often eccentric. He assumed this was just another quirk—perhaps some new encryption algorithm, or some academic project.

"Well, suit yourself." Mike stood up, patting Jiang Xinyu on the shoulder. "But don't push yourself too hard. Health comes first."

After Mike left, Jiang Xinyu turned back to the screen.

On the screen was the Bitcoin Forum (Bitcointalk), the most active discussion hub in the Bitcoin community at the time, and the very place where Satoshi Nakamoto had originally published the whitepaper. Jiang Xinyu had been browsing as a guest for a long time, watching the heated discussions about mining, trading, and technological development.

He noticed that the atmosphere in the community was shifting. As Bitcoin's price rose, more and more people were paying attention to the project—no longer just a niche playground for cryptography geeks, but gradually attracting investors and speculators.

But Jiang Xinyu cared nothing for price. He read every discussion about mining algorithms carefully, analyzing the strengths and weaknesses of every existing solution. He noticed that a few people in the community had already proposed the idea of ASIC miners, but most believed it was either technically infeasible or economically unviable.

"They're wrong." Jiang Xinyu murmured to himself. "ASIC is not only feasible—it's inevitable. The question is: who will do it first?"

An idea grew increasingly clear in his mind—he needed to establish his own identity, to make his voice heard in this community. If he could build a reputation in the community, perhaps he could find like-minded partners, or even investors.

Late one night in January 2012, a rare blizzard descended on New Haven. Jiang Xinyu sat alone in the laboratory, the fluorescent lights humming with a faint electrical buzz.

He opened the Bitcoin Forum's registration page, staring at the username field, lost in thought.

What should he call himself? He needed an ID that was distinctive yet wouldn't expose his real identity.

His gaze fell on the stuffed toy cat in the corner of the laboratory. It was a memento left by his ex-girlfriend, Amy. They had been in love for three years, but eventually parted ways due to differences in their life trajectories. Amy returned to California; he stayed on at Yale.

The cat was a chubby orange kitten whose fur had grown somewhat worn from being held so often while he thought.

Friedcat. 烤猫.

After completing registration, Jiang Xinyu posted his first message:

> Hello everyone, I am a chip design researcher currently studying computer science at Yale University. I have noticed that Bitcoin mining currently relies primarily on CPUs and GPUs, with low efficiency and enormous energy consumption. I believe ASIC chips are the way of the future, and I am researching related technical solutions. I hope to exchange ideas with technically-minded members of the community.

In the early days after the post was published, it didn't attract much attention. After all, in early 2012, ASIC miners were still just a concept in the minds of a few technical enthusiasts; most people believed GPU mining was already sufficiently efficient.

But Jiang Xinyu did not lose heart. He began posting frequently on the forum, sharing his thoughts on chip design, analyzing the flaws of existing mining methods, and sketching out the technical blueprint for ASIC miners. He supported every viewpoint with meticulous calculations and data; every post was carefully prepared and packed with technical substance.

"Current GPU mining efficiency is approximately 1–2 Mhash/J, whereas in theory, an ASIC chip fabricated using 130-nanometer (nm) process technology could achieve an efficiency of 200–300 Mhash/J. If we adopt more advanced processes, such as 55nm, efficiency could even break through 1000 Mhash/J. This means the same electrical input could produce hundreds of times more hashrate."

"More importantly, the specialization of ASICs will fundamentally transform the Bitcoin mining industry's structure. Individual miners will be unable to compete with specialized mining farms; mining will shift from a hobby to an industrialized production activity. This will be a multi-billion-dollar emerging industry."

These viewpoints seemed so far ahead of their time that they were dismissed as fantasy. Many people mocked him in the comments:

"Just another armchair theoretician."

"ASIC? Do you have any idea how much a tapeout costs? You couldn't sell yourself for enough."

"Study hard and stop daydreaming."

But Jiang Xinyu was unmoved. He knew that true innovation is often born in the solitude of being misunderstood.

Gradually, his persistence began to pay off. Some people with genuine technical backgrounds started paying attention to his posts, engaging in in-depth discussions with him. Among them, a user named "Bitfily" caught Jiang Xinyu's attention.

Bitfily claimed to be an engineer at a certain chip foundry with actual tapeout experience. They exchanged multiple in-depth technical discussions on the forum—chip architecture, cooling solutions, power management, mass production processes.

"Your design proposal is theoretically feasible," Bitfily said in a private message, "but the risks of actual tapeout are substantial. The 130nm process, while not cutting-edge, still carries a high cost for a startup project. Have you considered first building an FPGA prototype for verification?"

"FPGA efficiency gains are limited and cannot demonstrate ASIC's advantages." Jiang Xinyu replied. "And the market window won't wait. If we don't launch ASIC products soon, someone else will seize the initiative."

"What about funding? Do you have investors?"

"Not yet." Jiang Xinyu admitted. "But I'm considering a crowdfunding approach. There are many people in the Bitcoin community who believe in this technology. If they can become shareholders, it not only solves the funding problem but also builds a loyal user base."

"Crowdfunding?" Bitfily seemed intrigued by the idea. "That's a bold move. If it actually succeeds, ASICMiner will become the first community-crowdfunded mining hardware project in Bitcoin history."

And so, the concept of the ASICMiner project gradually took shape. Jiang Xinyu knew this would be a enormous gamble. But he also believed that only those who dare to take risks can change the world.

---

### III. The Concept of Crowdfunding

Spring 2012, New Haven. Cherry blossoms were in full bloom. Pink petals drifted on the wind, carpeting the campus paths. Students gathered in small groups on the lawns, enjoying the rare fine weather.

But Jiang Xinyu had no time to admire the scenery. He shut himself in the laboratory, surrounded by a thick stack of technical documents. After months of research, he had completed the initial design plan for ASICMiner—a Bitcoin mining chip using 130nm process technology.

"130nm……" he murmured, staring at the circuit diagram before him. "While not the most advanced process, the cost is controllable and the risk is manageable—suitable for a first tapeout."

According to his calculations, designing and tapeouting a batch of 130nm ASIC chips would require approximately 1 million RMB in funding. For an individual, that was a princely sum, but if raised through crowdfunding from the community, it was far from impossible.

At the time, 1 million RMB was approximately $160,000 USD. Within the Bitcoin community, while most investors were small-scale, the power of aggregation was not to be underestimated. Moreover, if successful, this would be a first in Bitcoin history—the first community-crowdfunded hardware project.

He opened the Bitcoin forum, took a deep breath, and began writing a post that would change his fate.

He spent an entire three days composing this post. Every word was carefully weighed; every data point was repeatedly verified.

---

**[Title] ASICMiner — Professional Bitcoin Mining Chip Crowdfunding Plan**

Fellow members of the Bitcoin community:

I am Friedcat (烤猫), a researcher specializing in chip design, currently studying computer science at Yale University. Over the past year, I have been researching optimization solutions for Bitcoin mining. Through in-depth technical analysis, I firmly believe that ASIC chips represent the ultimate form of Bitcoin mining.

Today, I formally present the ASICMiner project—a professional Bitcoin mining chip project designed and manufactured by a professional team. Our goal is to produce professional miners with energy efficiency far surpassing GPUs, and to allow community members to share in the dividends of this technological revolution through crowdfunding.

**Technical Specifications:**
- Process Node: 130nm CMOS
- Expected Efficiency: 200–300 Mhash/J (compared to GPU's 1–2 Mhash/J, a 100–300x improvement)
- Hashrate per Chip: approximately 300 Mhash/s
- Target Power Consumption: below 2W per chip
- Package Type: Quad Flat No-leads (QFN-64)
- Operating Temperature: 0°C to 70°C

**Financing Plan:**
- Target Amount: 1,000,000 RMB (approximately $160,000 USD)
- Equity Structure: Investors hold shares in ASICMiner company in proportion to their contributions
- Minimum Investment: 1 BTC (worth approximately $50–100 USD at the time)
- Profit Distribution: Chip sales revenue and self-operated mining revenue distributed proportionally per quarter

**Use of Funds:**
- Chip design and tapeout costs: 60%
- Miner assembly and testing costs: 20%
- Operations and personnel costs: 10%
- Reserved risk fund: 10%

**Team Introduction:**
- Project Lead: Friedcat (烤猫), PhD candidate in Computer Science at Yale University, with years of chip design experience, having participated in the design and tapeout of multiple commercial chips
- Technical Advisor: Bitfily, senior engineer at a well-known chip foundry with extensive ASIC design experience
- Planned Recruitment: 2 digital circuit engineers, 1 firmware engineer, 1 hardware engineer

**Project Timeline:**
- August 2012: Complete crowdfunding, funds secured
- September 2012: Register company in Shenzhen, assemble team
- October–December 2012: Complete chip design
- January 2013: Submit for tapeout
- March 2013: Receive samples, begin testing
- April 2013: Begin mass production, deliver first miners

**Risk Disclosures:**
1. There are technical risks associated with chip tapeout; it may not succeed on the first attempt and a second tapeout may be necessary. If the first tapeout fails, we will use the reserved funds for a second attempt. If the second attempt also fails, remaining funds will be refunded to investors proportionally.
2. Bitcoin price volatility risk. Significant drops in Bitcoin's price may affect miner sales and demand.
3. Mining difficulty increase risk. As network hashrate grows, individual miner output may decline.
4. Policy risk. Various governments' attitudes toward digital currencies may affect market development.

However, I believe the value of technological innovation will ultimately be recognized by the market. The emergence of ASIC miners will completely transform the Bitcoin mining landscape, and we will become the pioneers of this revolution.

If you share this vision, please contact us through the following channels to participate in the crowdfunding:
- Forum Private Message: Friedcat
- Email: friedcat.asicminer@protonmail.com
- Internet Relay Chat (IRC): #asicminer on Freenode

Let us together usher in the ASIC era of Bitcoin mining.

Friedcat
July 15, 2012

---

The moment the post went live, Jiang Xinyu's heart pounded like a war drum. He had no idea what response this bold venture would receive.

He stood up and walked to the window. Beyond it, the cherry blossoms were in full bloom, pink petals dancing in the wind. He thought of the message Satoshi Nakamoto had left in the Genesis Block:

"The Times 03/Jan/2009 Chancellor on brink of second bailout for banks"

It was a taunt at the traditional financial system, and a declaration of a new world. And now, Jiang Xinyu was continuing that revolutionary torch in his own way.

---

## Part One: The Crowdfunding Legend — The Birth of ASICMiner (2012)

### I. The First Battle Cry

July 2012. On the surface, the Bitcoin community was calm, but beneath the tranquility churned hidden currents.

Satoshi Nakamoto's disappearance had occurred over a year prior. Bitcoin's price hovered around $10, far from the dizzying heights it would later reach. But for those who truly understood the value of this technology, it was a moment before dawn.

In the first few days after Jiang Xinyu's crowdfunding post was published, the response was not enthusiastic.

"Another scammer, right?" someone mocked in the comments. "1 million RMB? Talk is cheap. How do we know you're not planning to run off with the money?"

"ASIC miners? Sounds great on paper, but who knows if it can actually be built? If the tapeout fails, the money goes down the drain."

"130nm process is way too outdated. Phone chips use 22nm nowadays. Using such ancient technology for miners—is this some kind of joke?"

Doubt piled upon doubt. In the world of cryptocurrency, scammers were everywhere, and people's vigilance had been honed to razor sharpness.

But Jiang Xinyu had been mentally prepared. He chose not to argue or fight back; instead, he responded to doubts with ever more technical details.

"Regarding the choice of 130nm process," he wrote in a reply, "I understand everyone's concerns. Indeed, 130nm is not considered advanced in semiconductor manufacturing, with the most cutting-edge processes now reaching 22nm or even 14nm. However, for the specific application of Bitcoin mining, 130nm is more than sufficient."

"More importantly, we must consider cost control and risk management. Tapeout costs for advanced processes grow exponentially—22nm tapeouts can exceed tens of millions of dollars, while 130nm costs only a few hundred thousand RMB. For a startup project, choosing a mature process dramatically reduces risk. Once the 130nm solution is validated, we can rapidly upgrade to more advanced process nodes."

He even disclosed his background—Special Class for the Gifted Young at USTC, Yale PhD candidate—and enthusiastic community members verified his student status through Yale's public directory.

The turning point came on the tenth day after the post went live.

A user named "jihad" replied: "I invested 1 BTC. It's not much, but I believe in the power of technological innovation. Friedcat, go for it!"

This reply seemed to open a floodgate. More and more community members began investing small amounts—some investing 0.5 BTC, others 2 BTC. What moved Jiang Xinyu even more was that many people included words of encouragement with their transfers.

"This is the beginning of a revolution—I'm counting on you!"

"Technology changes the world. Can't wait for your ASIC miners!"

Jiang Xinyu updated the crowdfunding progress every day, transparently publishing the status of funds received and project developments. This openness and honesty gradually earned the community's trust.

---

### II. The Appearance of Wu Jihan

August 2012, Beijing.

Beijing in summer was sweltering and boisterous. But in a quiet café in Wudaokou, the cool air from the air conditioning offered a touch of comfort.

Two people were engaged in a conversation that would change their destinies.

"So you're Friedcat?"

The speaker was a young man in his late twenties, tall and thin, with sharp eyes that betrayed a composure beyond his years. He wore a simple white shirt and dark trousers, looking like any ordinary white-collar worker. But Jiang Xinyu could sense something different about this person—a kind of confidence, a sense of control, a clear understanding of the world.

This was Wu Jihan, a graduate of Peking University with dual degrees in economics and psychology, who was at the time working at a well-known investment firm. Unlike Jiang Xinyu, the tech hermit, Wu Jihan was a quintessential business elite—he understood finance, understood the market, understood human nature.

"That's me." Jiang Xinyu nodded. He wore a plain T-shirt and jeans, a backpack slung over his shoulders—dressed entirely like a student.

"You're Wu Jihan?"

"At your service." Wu Jihan smiled faintly. "I've been following your posts on the Bitcoin forum for a while. Honestly, at first I thought it was a scam—there are too many con artists in this space. But after reading your technical proposal, I changed my mind."

Jiang Xinyu listened quietly, waiting for more.

"I've studied Bitcoin's economic model," Wu Jihan continued. "I believe this technology will completely transform the monetary system. It's not just a new payment method—it's a financial revolution."

"But the current mining efficiency is far too low, seriously constraining the network's development. CPU and GPU mining are squandering enormous amounts of electricity and computing resources—the cost structure is completely unsustainable. Your ASIC proposal is precisely the way to break through this bottleneck."

A flash of surprise crossed Jiang Xinyu's eyes. He had met many people who were interested in Bitcoin, but few could pinpoint the critical bottleneck in technological development as incisively as Wu Jihan.

"Are you interested in investing?" Jiang Xinyu asked directly.

"More than interested." Wu Jihan pulled a document from his bag and slid it across to Jiang Xinyu. It was a beautifully printed investment intent letter. "I've prepared 500,000 RMB. I'd like to become ASICMiner's largest individual shareholder."

Jiang Xinyu took the document and examined it carefully. 500,000 RMB meant Wu Jihan alone was contributing nearly half of the target fundraising amount.

"Why?" Jiang Xinyu looked up, meeting Wu Jihan's gaze directly. "Why trust someone you've never met?"

Wu Jihan smiled and set down his coffee cup.

"Because I saw something in you—faith." He said slowly. "Not chasing quick profits, but truly believing that technology can change the world. I've read all your posts on the forum—your technical analysis, your idealism, your commitment to decentralization…… none of it was put on."

"I found that same faith in myself. We might be the same kind of people—both believing that Bitcoin is not merely a speculative tool, but a genuine revolution."

"There's another reason," Wu Jihan added. "I've looked into your background. USTC Special Class for the Gifted Young, Yale PhD, years of research experience in chip design. Technical strength is hard currency; in this space, it's more persuasive than anything else."

A faint smile finally appeared on Jiang Xinyu's face.

"Alright." He extended his hand. "Welcome to ASICMiner, Mr. Wu Jihan."

The two hands clasped firmly.

---

### III. The Frenzy of Crowdfunding

Wu Jihan's investment was like a boulder dropped into a still lake, sending enormous ripples outward.

News spread rapidly through the Bitcoin community—"A big shot named Wu Jihan invested in Friedcat's ASICMiner project, they say he put in 500,000 RMB!" "I heard Wu Jihan came from Goldman Sachs, with killer instincts. If he's in, the project must be legit!"

This "influencer endorsement" effect was particularly pronounced in the age of social media. Investors who had been sitting on the sidelines rushed to follow suit, and the crowdfunding amount began growing at a visible pace.

800,000…… 900,000…… 1,000,000!

In mid-August 2012, ASICMiner's crowdfunding target was reached ahead of schedule, reaching a total of 1.2 million RMB—20% over the goal.

But Jiang Xinyu didn't stop fundraising there. He saw the market's enthusiasm and the project's potential. With the consent of the core team, he decided to keep the campaign open, ultimately raising approximately 2 million RMB in total funding.

"This money will be used as follows:" Jiang Xinyu published a detailed breakdown of fund usage on the forum. "First, chip design and tapeout costs—approximately 60% of total funds, about 1.2 million RMB; second, miner assembly and testing costs—approximately 20%, about 400,000 RMB; third, operations and personnel costs—approximately 10%, about 200,000 RMB; fourth, reserved risk funds—approximately 10%, to cover possible contingencies."

In September 2012, ASICMiner officially registered as a company in Shenzhen, under the Chinese name "比特泉科技有限公司" (BitSpring Technology Co., Ltd.). Jiang Xinyu served as CEO and Chief Technology Officer; Wu Jihan became the company's largest individual shareholder and took on the role of Strategic Advisor.

"BitSpring……" Jiang Xinyu stood in a modest office in Shenzhen, looking at the newly hung company sign, his heart full of mixed emotions.

From the Yale laboratory to Shenzhen's startup incubator; from the ivory tower of academic research to the brutal arena of commerce—once this step was taken, there was no turning back.

"Any regrets?" Wu Jihan stood beside him and asked.

"Regrets about what?"

"Dropping out of your PhD program to do this full-time." Wu Jihan said. "Over at Yale, I hear your advisor is furious. He probably won't hold your place for you."

Jiang Xinyu fell silent for a moment. His advisor had indeed strenuously opposed the decision, believing he was squandering his talent, chasing a bubble.

"When Satoshi created Bitcoin, nobody understood him either." Jiang Xinyu finally said. "True innovation is often born in the solitude of being misunderstood."

"Well said. Then let's make history together."

---

### IV. Days and Nights of R&D

Chip design is an extraordinarily complex and precise endeavor, requiring teams to work tirelessly. It is like building a city in a space the size of a fingernail—every transistor, every wire must be designed with pinpoint accuracy.

Jiang Xinyu assembled a lean, capable technical team, most members coming from top domestic chip design companies and research institutes. They rented a modest office in Nanshan District, Shenzhen, and began the arduous work of research and development.

"The chip architecture has been finalized," Jiang Xinyu stood before a whiteboard, explaining the design plan to team members. "We are using a multi-core parallel architecture, with each chip integrating 48 SHA-256 computing cores, interconnected via a bus and sharing a memory controller."

"Each core is an independent hash computation unit, capable of processing different nonce values in parallel. The benefit of this design is flexibility—we can selectively enable or disable certain cores as needed to achieve the optimal balance between power consumption and performance."

"Power consumption control is the biggest challenge," a senior engineer raised his concern. His name was Li Gong, from a well-known chip foundry with over ten years of ASIC design experience. "If 48 cores run simultaneously, the heat output will be enormous. If thermal management isn't handled properly, the chip could overheat and burn out."

"That's why the thermal design must keep pace," Jiang Xinyu replied. "I plan to use low-power design techniques, adding Dynamic Voltage and Frequency Scaling (DVFS) modules to each core, automatically adjusting frequency and voltage based on load."

"Additionally, we will use QFN packaging, which has a large thermal pad on the bottom that can be directly soldered to the Printed Circuit Board (PCB) for heat dissipation through the PCB."

"Which foundry have you chosen?" another engineer asked.

"SMIC." Jiang Xinyu answered without hesitation. "While their process isn't as advanced as TSMC's, the cost-to-performance ratio is excellent, and communication is more convenient domestically. For a mature process like 130nm, SMIC's quality fully meets our needs."

When night fell and team members departed one by one, Jiang Xinyu sat alone in the office, staring at the circuit diagrams on the screen.

His eyes were bloodshot; his face bore the marks of exhaustion. On the screen, Verilog code—the hardware description language for chip design—scrolled past. Hundreds of thousands of lines of code, each one凝结着他的心血.

Beyond the window, Shenzhen's nightscape blazed with dazzling brilliance.

In November 2012, ASICMiner's first design iteration was finally complete. After countless simulations and verifications, Jiang Xinyu finally felt confident enough to submit the design files to SMIC.

"From submission to chip return, it takes approximately three months." The foundry engineer told him.

Three months. Jiang Xinyu silently calculated the timeline. This meant he wouldn't receive the first batch of chips until February 2013.

---

### V. The Birth of the First Block

February 2013, Shenzhen.

Just after the Spring Festival, the atmosphere in ASICMiner's office was tense yet exhilarated.

"The chips are back!"

As the courier package was opened, twenty brand-new ASIC chips lay quietly in anti-static boxes. They were no bigger than a fingernail, their black封装 surface printed with the words "ASICMiner GEN1."

Jiang Xinyu carefully picked one up and held it under the light, examining it closely. The tiny silicon die gleamed with a metallic sheen in the sunlight.

"Begin packaging tests."

Over the following days, the team worked around the clock. Chip packaging, PCB soldering, firmware flashing, system debugging……

February 14, 2013. Valentine's Day.

ASICMiner's first prototype miner was finally assembled. It was a rudimentary device—exposed circuit boards, tangled cables, humming cooling fans, all fixed to a simple metal frame.

"Connect to the network, start the mining program."

Logs began scrolling across the screen:

```
Connecting to pool...
Connected to stratum+tcp://pool.bitcoin.com:3333
Initializing hardware...
Hardware initialized. 48 cores online.
Starting mining...
```

Then, the miracle happened.

```
Share accepted! Difficulty: 1
Share accepted! Difficulty: 1
Share accepted! Difficulty: 2
...
```

"We did it!"

Jiang Xinyu stared intently at the hashrate data on the screen—380 Mhash/s, exceeding the projected target by 20%!

But the real test was yet to come. They needed this miner to mine the first block of their own.

3:17 AM, February 28, 2013.

Jiang Xinyu sat alone in the office. Suddenly, the logs on the screen changed:

```
New block detected!
Hash: 0000000000000234a...
Difficulty: 3,275,465
*** FOUND BLOCK ***
Block height: 223456
Reward: 25 BTC
```

Jiang Xinyu leapt to his feet, hands trembling as they gripped the monitor.

"We found it…… we found it……"

This was ASICMiner's first mined block—the first block ever mined by a Chinese-developed ASIC miner. In Bitcoin's history, this was a milestone moment.

Jiang Xinyu picked up his phone and dialed Wu Jihan.

"We succeeded."

After hanging up, Jiang Xinyu walked to the window. Beyond it, Shenzhen's sky was beginning to lighten with the pale hues of dawn; a new day was about to begin.

And ASICMiner's new era officially opened at this very moment.

---

## Part Two: The Hashrate Empire — Mass Production of Miners and Dominion Over Hashrate (2013)

### I. The Birth of the Iron Flood

Spring 2013. A restless energy hung in the Shenzhen air.

ASICMiner's success was like a stone cast into a lake, the ripples spreading rapidly. The mining of the first block not only validated the technical feasibility—more importantly, it proved the gamble was worth continuing.

Jiang Xinyu stood in BitSpring Company's new office—they had moved out of the original incubator and leased an entire floor of office space.

The sales of the first batch of miners exceeded everyone's expectations. After news spread, orders poured in like snowflakes. Buyers from around the world—from China to the United States, from Russia to Iceland—all scrambling to get their hands on this revolutionary product.

"The first mass production order is confirmed." Wu Jihan walked into the office, carrying a thick stack of contracts. "We need to deliver five thousand miners within three months."

"Five thousand……" Jiang Xinyu frowned. "Can the production capacity keep up?"

"That's exactly what I want to discuss with you." Wu Jihan set the contracts on the desk. "The current contract manufacturing model is too inefficient. We need to establish our own production line."

"Where does the capital come from?"

"Additional financing." Wu Jihan's answer was terse and direct. "The profits from the first batch of miners have proven the business model's viability. Now there are many more investors wanting to get in. Sequoia Capital, IDG, ZhenFund—all have expressed interest. My initial estimate is we can raise another 50 to 100 million RMB."

"Are you sure about continuing to expand?" Jiang Xinyu asked. "Bitcoin price volatility is significant; if the price drops……"

"If the price drops, we ramp up self-operated mining." Wu Jihan cut him off. "Xinyu, you must understand—in this industry, hashrate is power. Whoever controls the hashrate controls the discourse."

"We are not merely selling miners; we are building a hashrate empire."

Jiang Xinyu fell silent. He thought of Satoshi Nakamoto's mention of "one CPU, one vote" in the whitepaper. But reality was clearly veering away from that vision. The emergence of ASIC miners would inevitably drive mining toward centralization.

"Alright." He finally nodded. "But we must control the pace. We cannot sacrifice quality for the sake of expansion."

"Rest assured, quality is your domain—I will not interfere." Wu Jihan laughed. "I'll just handle the money."

---

### II. The Roar of the Factory

May 2013, Dongguan.

BitSpring Company's first production line officially commenced operations in the Dongguan Songshan Lake High-Tech Industrial Development Zone. It was a 5,000-square-meter modern factory floor, equipped with state-of-the-art Surface Mount Technology (SMT) placers and automated testing equipment.

Dressed in anti-static work clothes, Jiang Xinyu stood beside the production line, watching as ASIC chips were precisely soldered onto circuit boards. The SMT machines emitted rhythmic clicking sounds; robotic arms precisely placed tiny components onto the PCBs.

"Production capacity is three hundred units per hour," the factory manager reported to him. "If we run three shifts, monthly capacity can reach two hundred thousand units."

"What about the yield rate?"

"Currently stable at over 97%."

He walked to the testing area, watching workers connect freshly下线 miners to power supplies for burn-in testing. Hundreds of miners ran simultaneously, the roar of cooling fans deafening.

"This is the iron flood." Jiang Xinyu murmured to himself.

In the conference room, Wu Jihan was conducting a video conference with several investors.

"……Yes, our Series B financing is complete, totaling 80 million RMB." Wu Jihan noticed Jiang Xinyu entering and gestured for him to sit. "This round was led by Sequoia Capital, with IDG and ZhenFund participating."

"With this capital, we plan to further expand production capacity and launch the development of the next-generation chip." Wu Jihan continued. "Our goal is to account for over 30% of total network hashrate by year-end."

After the video conference ended, Wu Jihan turned to Jiang Xinyu.

"Did you hear that? 30% of total network hashrate!" he said. "That means we will become the de facto guardians of the Bitcoin network."

"It also means enormous responsibility." Jiang Xinyu responded coolly. "If our hashrate becomes too concentrated, it threatens the network's decentralization characteristics."

"Theoretically, yes. But in reality, competition never stops." Wu Jihan said. "Even if we don't expand, others will. Bitmain, Canaan Creative—they're all circling."

"How is the next-generation chip design progressing?" Wu Jihan asked.

"Already underway." Jiang Xinyu replied. "This time we're using 55nm process, targeting energy efficiency above 1,000 Mhash/J and single-chip hashrate exceeding 10 Ghash/s."

"Excellent. Then let the iron flood keep rolling."

---

### III. The Grandeur of the Mine

Summer 2013, Ordos, Inner Mongolia.

On the vast grasslands, a massive industrial facility was rising. This was the ultra-large mine that BitSpring Company had invested in building—a planned installed capacity of 50 megawatts, capable of accommodating hundreds of thousands of miners running simultaneously.

Jiang Xinyu stood atop a small hill, surveying the construction site before him.

"Why did you choose this location?"

"Three reasons." Wu Jihan held up three fingers. "First, cheap electricity. Ordos's thermal power price is just over two mao per kilowatt-hour—less than one-third of coastal areas. Second, cool climate. The grassland's low average annual temperature can dramatically reduce cooling costs. Third, policy support. The local government has identified the big data industry as a key priority and has offered us numerous incentives."

"When can production begin?"

"The first phase of 10 megawatts can begin operations in September. At full capacity, it can produce over 100 bitcoins per day."

Jiang Xinyu quickly ran the numbers in his mind. At Bitcoin's price at the time, 100 bitcoins were worth approximately $100,000 USD—over $3 million USD per month in revenue.

"But this also means enormous risk." he said. "If Bitcoin's price plummets, or if mining difficulty surges……"

"That's why we need diversification." Wu Jihan cut him off. "In addition to self-operated mining, we must aggressively sell miners. Let others bear the risk of price fluctuations; we earn the sure money."

---

### IV. The Dominion of Hashrate

Autumn 2013. The Bitcoin world ushered in a historic moment.

With the full rollout of ASICMiner miners and the successive commissioning of self-operated mines, the proportion of total network hashrate controlled by BitSpring Company climbed rapidly.

10%…… 20%…… 30%……

By October 2013, this figure reached an astonishing 42%.

This meant that nearly one in every two Bitcoin blocks was mined by ASICMiner's machines. This kind of hashrate dominance was unprecedented in Bitcoin's history.

Discussions about ASICMiner on the Bitcoin forum continued to heat up.

"Friedcat is practically a god! Three months ago, who could have imagined that a crowdfunding project could grow to this scale?"

"42% hashrate…… this is insane. If they wanted to launch a 51% attack, theoretically they could."

"Don't worry, Friedcat isn't that kind of person."

"This is the paradox of decentralization—the technology that pursues decentralization is, in reality, moving toward centralization."

Jiang Xinyu read these discussions, his heart complicated.

He knew that the 42% hashrate share had already touched a dangerous boundary. If it exceeded 50%, the network's bookkeeping rights could theoretically be controlled; the so-called "51% attack" would become possible.

"We should proactively reduce our hashrate share." he proposed at a company meeting.

"What?" Everyone was stunned.

"I said, we should proactively reduce our hashrate share." Jiang Xinyu repeated. "We can pause some self-operated miners, or limit sales of new miners. Keep our share of total network hashrate below 30%."

"Are you insane?" Wu Jihan was the first to object. "Do you know what that means? It means giving up billions in profits!"

"But if I don't, how will the Bitcoin community see us? They'll view us as a threat. In the long run, that's more dangerous than losing some profits."

"I disagree." Wu Jihan said. "Market competition is brutal. If we voluntarily contract, others will fill the void. We'll end up losing both profits and market position."

The two clashed head-on; neither could convince the other.

In the end, the company continued to operate at maximum capacity, with hashrate share hovering around 42% for months.

"Friedcat is becoming the kind of person he once opposed—a monopolist." someone wrote on the forum.

When Jiang Xinyu saw these remarks, his heart was filled with bitterness.

"Power truly does corrupt people." he wrote in his diary. "But what corrupts people more than power is the pressure of maintaining it."

---

### V. The Myth of Wealth

November 2013. Bitcoin's price began an unprecedented surge.

From approximately $200 at the beginning of the month, it broke through $1,000 by month's end—a gain exceeding 400%.

"This month's financial statements are ready." The CFO walked into Jiang Xinyu's office, handing him a document. "You'd better sit down for this."

Jiang Xinyu took the report, his gaze falling on the net profit figure at the bottom—

**230 million RMB.**

His hand trembled slightly.

Three months. In just three months, BitSpring Company's net profit exceeded 200 million RMB.

"The speed of wealth accumulation is enough to suffocate." he wrote in his diary. "But is this money really ours? Or is it merely temporarily entrusted to us?"

"Wu Jihan wants more." he wrote. "He wants to go public, wants to become the world's largest miner manufacturer, wants to build a commercial empire. And me…… I just want to make a perfect chip."

The rift between them was quietly widening.

---

### VI. The Loneliness of the Emperor

December 2013, Shenzhen.

BitSpring Company held a grand annual dinner. In a 5,000-square-meter banquet hall sat the company's senior management, core engineers, investor representatives, and media guests.

Jiang Xinyu stood on stage, accepting the "Annual Leader of China's Blockchain Industry" trophy. Stage lights blazed down on him; applause surged like a tide.

After his speech, he descended the stage and was immediately swarmed by journalists and investors.

"Mr. Friedcat, what is your prediction for Bitcoin's future price?"

"There are rumors that ASICMiner plans to list in Hong Kong—can you confirm?"

Jiang Xinyu answered these questions mechanically, wearing a perfunctory smile.

After the banquet, he drove alone to the seashore.

Shenzhen Bay's night was tranquil and beautiful; Hong Kong's lights glittered across the water. Jiang Xinyu sat on the breakwater, letting the sea breeze wash over him.

"Is this what success feels like?" he asked himself.

42% hashrate share, 200 million RMB in monthly profits, a company valued at over $1 billion. To outsiders, he was already the king of this industry.

But what he felt was a bone-deep loneliness.

His phone rang—it was his wife.

"Xinyu, when are you coming home?"

"Soon. I'll head back after I wrap things up here."

"That's what you always say." Displeasure tinged his wife's voice. "You haven't been home for a month. Our daughter asks about Daddy every day."

Jiang Xinyu fell silent.

"I'm sorry." he said quietly. "I'll definitely come home this week."

After hanging up, he gazed at the lights across the water, his heart full of confusion.

"What am I actually chasing?"

---

## Part Three: The Pinnacle — 42% Hashrate and the Wealth Myth (2013–2014)

### I. The Throne of Hashrate

January 2014, Beijing.

A conference titled "China Bitcoin Industry Summit" was held at a five-star hotel.

Jiang Xinyu was the keynote speaker of the event. When he walked onto the podium, the entire audience rose to their feet in applause.

"Good morning, everyone." Jiang Xinyu nodded slightly. "Today, what I want to share is not ASICMiner's success story, but some reflections on the industry's future."

He pulled up a slide displaying a global Bitcoin hashrate distribution map.

"As you can see, China's share of global hashrate has exceeded 70%. This is a number we can be proud of, but it also places enormous responsibility on our shoulders."

"Bitcoin's core philosophy is decentralization, but in reality, hashrate is inevitably becoming concentrated. This is no one's fault—it is the inevitable result of technological development. Specialized, large-scale mining will inevitably defeat individual miners. The question is: when hashrate becomes too concentrated, the network's security is threatened."

"So, I solemnly pledge here: ASICMiner will never pursue a hashrate share exceeding 50%. We will proactively limit our own scale, leaving space for other miners and mining pools."

This speech drew warm applause. But deep down, Jiang Xinyu knew this promise would not be easy to keep.

---

### II. The Prelude to a Split

February 2014, Shenzhen.

An acrimonious board meeting was underway in BitSpring Company's conference room.

"I strongly oppose listing in Hong Kong!" Jiang Xinyu slammed the table and rose. "Listing now means handing the company's future over to the capital markets. What do those investors know about technology? They only care about next quarter's earnings report!"

"But the company needs capital to expand!" Wu Jihan retorted. "Competitors are catching up—Bitmain has completed its Series A financing. If we don't accelerate, we'll be overtaken!"

"Then let them overtake me! I want to make the best products, not the biggest company!"

"You're being too idealistic! In this industry, you either move forward or you fall behind. You think technological superiority can keep you ahead forever? Wrong! Channels, brand, capital—these are what determine victory!"

The other directors in the conference room exchanged uneasy glances.

"I propose a vote." Wu Jihan cooled down. "Whether to launch the Hong Kong listing plan."

The voting result chilled Jiang Xinyu's heart—except for himself, nearly every director supported the listing plan.

"You……" He stared in disbelief at these former comrades-in-arms.

"Xinyu," an investor representative spoke up, "we respect your technical ideals, but at this stage of the company's development, we must consider investors' interests. Listing is the best exit route."

"I'm tired." He stood up. "Let's end today's meeting here."

With that, he turned and walked out of the conference room.

---

### III. The Calm Before the Storm

March 2014, Shenzhen.

The spring sun was warm and gentle, but Jiang Xinyu's mood was as cold as ice. Since that board meeting, his relationship with Wu Jihan had plummeted to its lowest point.

Worse, the company's operating conditions had begun to deteriorate. Bitcoin's price, after peaking at $1,000 in December 2013, had been in continuous decline. By March 2014, it had fallen to around $600. Miner sales were severely impacted; many customers cancelled their orders.

"Severe inventory backlog and tight capital chain." The CFO reported to him. "If we can't return capital quickly, the company may face a liquidity crisis."

"Pause the expansion plan and cut operational costs."

A few days later, an article titled "ASICMiner Infighting: The Collision of Technical Ideals and Commercial Reality" circulated online. The article detailed the disagreements between Jiang Xinyu and Wu Jihan, hinting that the company might face a split.

"Who leaked this?" Jiang Xinyu demanded at an emergency meeting.

No one answered. Everyone's eyes quietly glanced toward Wu Jihan.

"Xinyu," Wu Jihan spoke up, "I think you need to take a rest. Your state lately has not been good, and it's had a negative impact on the company's development."

"What do you mean?"

"What I mean is, you should consider taking a temporary leave from the company and adjusting your mindset."

Jiang Xinyu was struck like lightning. He understood—this was Wu Jihan's countermove.

"Fine." Jiang Xinyu gave a hollow laugh. "I'm leaving."

He stood up, removed his employee badge from his chest, and threw it onto the table.

"But I want you all to remember—ASICMiner was founded by me. Without me, you're nothing."

With that, he turned and walked out of the conference room.

---

### IV. The New Mine and the Flash Marriage

April 2014, Sichuan.

After leaving ASICMiner, Jiang Xinyu journeyed alone to Sichuan. This province was China's hydropower powerhouse, and its abundant water resources had attracted numerous Bitcoin mines.

"I don't want to be controlled by others anymore." he told his newly recruited team members. "This time, I will do things my way."

"Our goal is to build a green mine." Jiang Xinyu said at the planning meeting. "Powered entirely by hydropower, achieving carbon neutrality. At the same time, we will develop a new generation of low-power miners, breaking existing efficiency records."

In May 2014, the new mine project officially launched. The site was chosen near a hydroelectric power station in Ganzi Prefecture, Sichuan Province, with a planned installed capacity of 20 megawatts.

Just as the project was in full swing, shocking news stunned the entire team.

Jiang Xinyu was getting married. The bride was a woman named Wang Li; the two had known each other for less than a month.

"Boss, isn't this a bit hasty?"

But Jiang Xinyu appeared calm.

"Maybe." he said. "But I need someone to be with me. I've had enough of the loneliness all these years."

The wedding was simple, with only a few close friends in attendance. The bride, Wang Li, was young and beautiful, gentle and lovely to look at.

---

## Part Four: The Phantom Vanishes — The Mystery of the Disappearance and the Evaporation of Funds (2014–2015)

### I. The Shadow of the New Mine

June 2014, Ganzi, Sichuan.

Against the backdrop of majestic snow-capped mountains, a modern factory was rising from the earth. This was Jiang Xinyu's new mine—"BitSpring Phase II." Though the name still carried the old brand, it was now a completely independent entity.

"All equipment is in place; trial operations can begin next week." The project manager reported to Jiang Xinyu.

Jiang Xinyu nodded, but his attention seemed elsewhere. Ever since his marriage, his state had not been good; he often sat发呆 in his office alone.

"Mr. Jiang, are you alright?" The manager asked with concern.

"Fine." Jiang Xinyu managed a thin smile. "Just a bit tired."

Wang Li had also come to Sichuan. She owned an apartment in Chengdu and would often return to the city for a few days.

But what he didn't know was that a conspiracy was quietly unfolding.

---

### II. The Evaporation of Funds

July 2014. The new mine officially commenced operations.

The first batch of five thousand miners began running, their enormous power draw making nearby transformers groan in distress.

However, the good times were short-lived.

In early August 2014, the finance manager rushed to Jiang Xinyu with a panicked expression.

"Mr. Jiang, something terrible happened! Most of the funds in the company's account…… are gone!"

Jiang Xinyu was struck numb.

"What? That's impossible!"

"It's true." The finance staff member handed over a bank statement with trembling hands. "Over the past two months, multiple large transfers were made—totaling over 80 million RMB. The recipients were…… personal accounts."

Jiang Xinyu took the statement. His hands shook. He had no knowledge whatsoever of these transfers.

"Where's Wang Li?" It suddenly occurred to him. "Where is she?"

"Ms…… Ms. Wang hasn't come to the company for a week." The finance staff member's expression grew strange. "And…… those receiving accounts seem to be connected to her."

The world spun around Jiang Xinyu.

---

### III. The Wife's Lies

Police investigation quickly yielded results.

Wang Li's identity was false. She was by no means an investor; she was a professional con artist. Her real name was Li Mei, and she had multiple prior convictions for marriage fraud. She specifically targeted wealthy single men, marrying them quickly to steal their assets.

The funds she had embezzled had already been transferred overseas through various channels and were nearly impossible to recover.

"Mr. Jiang, we regret to inform you," the detective in charge said. "The suspect has fled the country, and her current whereabouts are unknown. We are seeking assistance through INTERPOL, but the prospects for recovering the funds are slim."

Jiang Xinyu sat in the police station chair, experiencing a kind of despair he had never felt before.

80 million RMB. That was all the operating capital for the new mine—the hard-earned money that investors had trusted him with.

What was even harder to accept was that he himself had brought the wolf through the door.

"I'm such a fool." he murmured to himself.

---

### IV. Liquidation and Collapse

News of the embezzlement spread rapidly through the industry. Investors came demanding repayment; suppliers demanded immediate payment; the bank froze the company's accounts.

In September 2014, BitSpring Phase II officially declared bankruptcy and entered liquidation. The court seized the mine's equipment; proceeds from the auction were used to repay debts. But the debt hole was far too large—total liabilities exceeded 100 million RMB.

The court ultimately ruled that Jiang Xinyu bore personal joint liability and was required to repay investors' losses exceeding 50 million RMB.

---

### V. The Divorce Verdict

January 2015, Chaoyang District People's Court, Beijing.

Jiang Xinyu stood in the defendant's seat, listening to the judge read the verdict.

"In the case of plaintiff Wang Li (real name Li Mei) suing defendant Jiang Xinyu for divorce…… this court holds that the plaintiff, during the marriage with the defendant, engaged in the act of defrauding the defendant's company funds, constituting a suspected criminal offense. The marriage between the plaintiff and defendant was established on a basis of fraud and shall, in accordance with the law, be determined invalid."

"The verdict is as follows: 1. The marriage between the plaintiff and defendant is declared invalid; 2. The plaintiff's claim for division of property is dismissed; 3. Court costs shall be borne by the plaintiff."

The gavel fell. Silence filled the courtroom.

Jiang Xinyu walked out of the courthouse. The winter sun was pale and harsh. This marriage, lasting less than a year, had ended in the most absurd way possible.

---

### VI. The Final Backside

Spring 2015, Beijing.

Jiang Xinyu stood on the pedestrian walkway in Terminal 3 of the Capital Airport, a simple suitcase behind him. He had bought a one-way ticket to a small, unknown city abroad.

"Are you really leaving?" the friend who had come to see him off asked.

"Yeah." Jiang Xinyu nodded. "There's nothing left for me here."

"What about ASICMiner? Are you going to abandon it?"

Jiang Xinyu forced a bitter smile. ASICMiner had long since had nothing to do with him. After his departure, Wu Jihan had, through a series of financial maneuvers, taken complete control of the company.

"That was no longer my ASICMiner."

He picked up his suitcase and turned toward security.

"Xinyu!" his friend called after him. "Will you ever come back?"

Jiang Xinyu stopped but did not turn around.

"Maybe." His voice was soft, drifting in the wind. "When everything has been forgotten."

Then he continued forward, his figure gradually disappearing into the flow of people.

And so, Friedcat—Jiang Xinyu—the once-formidable father of Bitcoin mining machines, vanished from public view in an almost absurd way.

---

## Epilogue: The Revival of the Dead (2018, 2024)

### I. The Dormant Addresses

Jiang Xinyu was gone, but his Bitcoin addresses remained on the blockchain. As ASICMiner's founder, he personally held a substantial amount of bitcoin. These coins were distributed across dozens of addresses and had remained dormant since his disappearance.

Within the community, speculation about these addresses' fate was frequent.

"Is Friedcat dead? Otherwise, why haven't these coins moved at all?"

"Maybe he just forgot his private keys."

Days turned to months, months to years. Bitcoin's price went through several bull and bear cycles. The value of the coins in those addresses grew from tens of millions of dollars to hundreds of millions, but no matter how prices fluctuated, these coins never moved.

---

### II. The Anomaly of 2018

January 2018. The Bitcoin community was stunned by news. A long-dormant Bitcoin address, believed to belong to Friedcat, suddenly executed an outbound transaction. The amount was small—just 50 BTC, worth approximately $500,000 at the time.

But the significance of this transaction was extraordinary—it proved that the address's owner, or whoever held the private keys, was still alive.

"Friedcat is alive!" The community erupted.

But the funds were quickly transferred to multiple exchanges, then exchanged for other cryptocurrencies, disappearing without a trace. After that, the address fell silent once again.

---

### III. The Phantom of 2024

March 2024. Bitcoin's price had broken through historical highs, reaching an astonishing $70,000.

Then, something even more staggering occurred. Multiple long-dormant addresses, believed to belong to Friedcat, executed large outbound transfers on the same day. In total, over 10,000 BTC—worth more than $700 million at prevailing prices.

"The dead have revived." someone wrote on the forum.

But as with the 2018 incident, these funds were rapidly dispersed across thousands of different addresses, then, through complex mixing operations, vanished into the vast ocean of anonymity.

No one could trace where the money ultimately went, and no one could locate Friedcat.

---

### IV. The Unsolved Mystery

To this day, the mystery of Friedcat remains unsolved.

Where did he go? Is he alive or dead? Are the movements of those Bitcoin addresses being made by him personally, or by someone who obtained his private keys?

No one knows the answer.

Like Satoshi Nakamoto's disappearance, Friedcat's vanishing has become one of the greatest unsolved mysteries in Bitcoin's history.

---

## Final Chapter: The Trajectory of a Shooting Star

Friedcat—Jiang Xinyu. This name has left an indelible mark on Bitcoin's history.

He was the father of ASIC mining in China, the first person to transform Bitcoin mining from a hobby into an industry. The ASICMiner he founded once commanded 42% of total network hashrate—a true hashrate hegemon.

But his story is also a tragedy about ideals versus reality, trust versus betrayal, rise and fall.

He trusted too much in technology, yet overlooked the complexity of human nature. He believed that superior chip design alone could secure a place in this world, yet ultimately was devoured by commercial ruthlessness and the darkness of human nature.

He was too lonely, too desperate for warmth—and as a result, fell into a con artist's trap, losing everything.

His disappearance left the world with too many questions and too much to lament.

Perhaps, in some unknown corner, he still lives, watching it all, remembering that night in the Yale laboratory when he first read the Bitcoin whitepaper.

Perhaps he is no longer of this world.

In any case, his story has become part of Bitcoin's legend, a microcosm of that wild era.

A story about genius, wealth, power, and destruction.

A story about a shooting star—burning so brilliantly, vanishing so suddenly.

---

> A technical genius blazed across the sky like a shooting star, igniting a mining revolution—only to plunge into the abyss at its peak. The fatal paradox of anonymity and trust; the "disappearances" in the crypto world haunt like ghosts, warning of greed's power to devour.
>
> When we gaze upon the starry sky, those vanished stars may yet be shining somewhere. The legend of Friedcat shall forever glitter in the eternal memory of the blockchain.

---

**— End of Chapter 4 —**
Chapter 1: The Dawn of Cryptography — From David Chaum to the Pioneers of Digital Gold

> *"Privacy is the power to selectively reveal oneself to the world."*
> *— David Chaum*

---

## Prologue: A Late Night in 1983

In 1983, President Reagan was signing the defense budget in the Oval Office; Apple launched the Lisa computer with a graphical interface; and the internet was still the exclusive domain of the military and academic institutions.

In an old building on the edge of the UC Berkeley campus, a thirty-two-year-old computer science doctoral student named David Chaum was staring at lines of code scrolling across his screen. His eyes were bloodshot, but the flames of idealism burned bright in his pupils.

Chaum's laboratory was in the basement, with no windows, the vents emitting a low, constant hum. The room smelled of old paper, electronic components, and cheap coffee mixed together. On shelves along the walls stood oscilloscopes, logic analyzers, modems, and an IBM personal computer — a luxury item at the time.

At this moment, Chaum sat in front of the IBM PC, the green glow of the fluorescent screen illuminating his pale face. On the screen was the code he had just completed — eCash, electronic cash.

"This is it," Chaum murmured to himself, his voice hoarse from hours of silence.

His fingers hovered over the keyboard, trembling slightly. He had been working continuously for forty-eight hours, sustained only by black coffee and energy bars. His body was exhausted, but his mind was extraordinarily alert.

Chaum took a deep breath and saved the code to a floppy disk. The disk drive emitted a harsh scratching sound as it read and wrote. The moment the indicator light went out, he knew that history had been rewritten.

"Anonymous digital payments," he said softly. "Real, untraceable, completely private electronic money. Not like credit cards — those leave transaction records that banks can monitor. Not like checks — those create payment trails that governments can follow. But like cash — once it changes hands, the transaction is complete, leaving no trace."

He stood up and paced around the cramped laboratory, his steps somewhat unsteady.

"In this digital age dawning upon us, without privacy protections, we will live in a panopticon. Every purchase will be recorded, every behavior monitored, every person as transparent as a glass mannequin."

He walked to the wall, where his fingers lightly touched a poster — the cover of George Orwell's 1984.

"But eCash will be a sword, severing the chains of surveillance."

Chaum walked to the teletype machine and began typing a message.

Recipient: cryptography mailing list (cryptography@list.enet.dec.com)
Subject: A New Electronic Payment Scheme — Electronic Cash (eCash)

"Fellow colleagues, I wish to share an idea with you. In this increasingly digital world, we face a fundamental question: How can we enjoy the convenience of electronic payments while protecting individual privacy and freedom?"

"Existing electronic payment systems — whether credit cards or electronic transfers — share a common flaw: they all rely on a Trusted Third Party (typically a bank or financial institution) to verify and record every transaction. This means this third party holds complete surveillance power over all transactions."

"I propose an entirely new scheme, which I call 'electronic cash.' At the core of this scheme is 'blind signature' technology — a technique that allows users to obtain a bank's valid signature on currency while the bank cannot know what it is signing and cannot trace where that currency ultimately flows."

He described the principles of blind signatures in detail: the user generates a random serial number, performs mathematical operations on it with the bank's public key ("blinding"), then sends the blinded data to the bank. The bank signs it with its private key and returns it to the user, who then performs the inverse operation ("unblinding"), ultimately obtaining a valid signature that the bank cannot trace.

"The result is this: the bank can verify that the money is genuine (because it carries the bank's valid signature), but it cannot know who the money was originally issued to, nor who ultimately spent it. Just like real cash — once withdrawn from the bank, its trail disappears into the vast sea of people."

Chaum pressed the send button. The teletype machine emitted a string of mechanical sounds, converting the message into electrical pulses that traveled through telephone lines to destinations thousands of miles away.

Then he stood up, walked to the sink, and splashed cold water on his haggard face. The mirror reflected a man with sunken eyes and stubble, but the light in those eyes still burned fiercely.

Outside the window, Berkeley's night sky was dotted with stars. In the summer of 1983, David Chaum had lit the first torch in the history of digital currency.

---

## Part One: The Flames of Idealism

### 1. Early Years: The Call of Cryptography

David Chaum was born in 1955 in a middle-class family in New York City. His father was an electrical engineer, and his mother was a middle school mathematics teacher. Though not wealthy, the family was filled with a deep respect for knowledge and education.

The young Chaum was not all that different from other children. He enjoyed taking apart radios, reading science fiction novels, and gazing at the stars. But he had an almost obsessive passion for mathematics.

"David has loved numbers since he was little," his mother recalled later. "He could sit in front of his building blocks for an entire day, not to build houses, but to study the relationships between geometric shapes."

This mathematical talent became increasingly evident as Chaum progressed through school. By high school, he had already self-studied university-level calculus and linear algebra. His math teacher said Chaum was one of the brightest students he had ever taught, "But he wasn't just smart — he had intuition. He could 'see' the things behind the mathematics, not just compute formulas."

In 1974, Chaum was admitted to the University of California, Berkeley with excellent grades, majoring in mathematics and computer science.

Berkeley at that time was one of the most radical and open academic centers in the United States. The aftermath of the anti-Vietnam War movement was still rippling through campus, the hippie culture was still flourishing, and the personal computer revolution was brewing in Silicon Valley. Young people questioned authority, pursued freedom, and believed that technology could change the world.

Chaum was quickly drawn to this atmosphere. But he was not a radical who took to the streets for protests; he chose a different path to pursue freedom — through mathematics and cryptography.

"I first encountered cryptography in 1976," Chaum recalled later. "That was when Whitfield Diffie and Martin Hellman published their famous paper, 'New Directions in Cryptography.' They proposed the concept of public-key cryptography, and it completely blew my mind."

That paper was indeed a milestone in the history of cryptography. Before that, cryptography was primarily the domain of the military, inaccessible to ordinary people.
